<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="cf292ac1803e46949540ee3318209626"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="cf292ac1803e46949540ee3318209626">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'bc5aeb80-85cf-4e88-bb72-92b60674eb4e'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://advanced.onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '223EDD9AF7700042DBFE150E18C8263C';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d47655017" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p><b>Double-walled Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> nanotubes</b> take full advantage of the high capacity of Sb, the good stability of TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, and their unique interaction, realizing excellent electrochemical performance both in lithium-ion batteries and sodium-ion batteries.

            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a4dbf267-79cf-4ad5-be85-2282c86e0609/adma201505918-gra-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a4dbf267-79cf-4ad5-be85-2282c86e0609/adma201505918-gra-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b19e43e2-4620-4e10-8c32-0aea7f8c761e/adma201505918-gra-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a4dbf267-79cf-4ad5-be85-2282c86e0609/adma201505918-gra-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201505918-sec-0010">
         
         <p>Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), as an efficient energy-storage device, are achieving great advances in many fields, such as portable electronics and electric vehicles, as well as smart grid systems. In spite of this, they still can not meet the ever-growing demand of electrical devices on energy and power. Thus, searching for novel electrode materials for high-capacity and high-power batteries becomes of paramount importance. In the case of anode materials, Sb is attracting extensive attention, due to its high theoretical capacity (660 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> or 1890 mA h cm<sup>−3</sup>),<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0001_R_d47655007e295" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Rev.">1</a></span> unique puckered-layer structures, small electrode polarization (≈0.2 V), and moderate working voltage (0.8–0.9 V). The puckered-layer structure in a low stacking density (≈39%), could benefit the fast diffusion of Li ions and the release of the structure strain, enhancing the rate capability and keeping the electrochemical stability.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0002_R_d47655007e297" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Environ. Sci.">2</a></span> The small polarization could reduce the self-heating effect upon cycling and the risk of thermal runaway. The moderate discharge voltage could avoid the formation of dendritic lithium crystals on the surface. Besides that, Sb is also a promising anode candidate for sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) that could effectively mitigate the strained lithium resources, particularly useful for large-scale energy storage.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0003_R_d47655007e299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev.">3</a></span> In spite of these advantages, the large volume variation of Sb both in LIBs (≈147%) and in NIBs (≈293%) is still a great challenge to its performances as an anode material,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0003_R_d47655007e302" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev.">3</a></span> because it is easy to induce the particle pulverization and the capacity degradation.</p>
         
         <p>In order to address this issue, Sb has been fabricated into hollow nanoparticles,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0004_R_d47655007e307" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Mater.">4</a></span> hybridized with carbon materials,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0005_R_d47655007e309" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Commun.">5</a>-<a href="#adma201505918-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0012_R_d47655007e309" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A">12</a></span> or blended with other elements to generate stable alloys like SnSb,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0013_R_d47655007e311" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nanoscale">13</a></span> BiSb,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0014_R_d47655007e313" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Mater.">14</a></span> etc. Although these strategies effectively improve their electrochemical performances, they are still far away from being satisfactory, neither in LIBs nor in NIBs. Recently, the appearance of oxygen-deficient TiO<sub>2</sub> (TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>) has shed light on high-performance Sb anodes, because it shows the promising potential as an interface material for high-capacity anode materials. The good electrochemical/thermal stability, the strong binding affinity to many electrode materials from TiO<sub>2</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0015_R_d47655007e324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></span> and the enhanced charge-transfer kinetics from oxygen vacancies<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0016_R_d47655007e326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Mater.">16</a></span> enable TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> to retain the structure/electrochemical stability without the deterioration of the transfer kinetics. The results have been validated on Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0015_R_d47655007e337" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></span> a typical conversion-type anode material, where Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> nanocomposites showed significantly enhanced electrochemical performance than Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, TiO<sub>2</sub>, and Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> without oxygen vacancies. In spite of this, whetherTiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> works as an interface material for anodes based on an alloy mechanism is still open to question. Therefore, Sb is selected as a model to explore this possibility.</p>
         
         <p>Here, double-walled crystalline Sb@amorphous TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> nanotubes are synthesized by the calcination of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> produced by the hydrolysis of tetrabutyl titanate (TBOT) on Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanorods. The formation of the double-walled structures could be assigned to the facile reduction of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and the strong interaction of Sb to TiO<sub>2</sub>. Such a structure accommodates the volume variation upon cycling well, then keeps the structure stable. Meanwhile, this calcination in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> also generates a lot of oxygen vacancies in the shell, supported by Ti<sup>3+</sup> species in XPS spectra. They would enhance the charge-transfer kinetics, particularly at the interface. Thus, the double-walled nanotubes show excellent electrochemical performance as an anode material in LIBs and in NIBs, both of which are on the top of the reported list.</p>
         
         <p>The synthesis process of double-walled Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> nanotubes is briefly described in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0007">1</a>. First, high-quality Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanorods (Figure S1, Supporting Information) were synthesized in a high yield by a typical solvothermal reaction.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0017_R_d47655007e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Cryst. Growth">17</a></span> The nanorods showed a narrow size distribution with diameters of ≈42 nm and lengths up to several micrometers. Then, the Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanorods were coated by a thin layer of amorphous TiO<sub>2</sub> (≈4 nm) via a moderate hydrolysis of tetrabutyl titanate (TBOT) at 45 °C, producing core–shell Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods (Figure S2, Supporting Information). The following calcination of these core–shell nanorods at 350 °C in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> led to the decomposition of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> to Sb and the partial reduction of Ti<sup>4+</sup> in TiO<sub>2</sub>. Due to the strong interaction between Sb and TiO<sub>2</sub>, the as-formed Sb nanocrystals were attached to the TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> shell, resulting in the formation of double-walled Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> nanotubes. This interaction also inhibits the evaporation of Sb during the calcination, and keeps the Sb electrochemically active as much as possible in the product. However, it promotes the decomposition of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> at a low temperature, based on thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) (Figure S3, Supporting Information). For the sake of clarity, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanorods, core–shell Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods, and double-walled Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> nanotubes are denoted as Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub>, and Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201505918-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/de2068f8-1f16-42e3-aae6-c1f68ac5351c/adma201505918-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/de2068f8-1f16-42e3-aae6-c1f68ac5351c/adma201505918-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/33d76035-716c-4fef-b5dc-4e2a345e7a8e/adma201505918-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/de2068f8-1f16-42e3-aae6-c1f68ac5351c/adma201505918-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201505918-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201505918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The preparation process of double-walled Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> nanotubes.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201505918-sec-0020">
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0001">1</a>a shows the X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>. It is noted that all the reflections come from metallic Sb (rhombohedral phase, JCPDS Card No, 35–0732). Those related to crystalline TiO<sub>2</sub> are completely absent, suggesting the amorphous nature of the TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> shell. This result could be attributed to the low calcination temperature during the preparation. In fact, such a TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> shell could be traced by Raman, X-ray photoelectron spectrometry (XPS), and energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS). As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0001">1</a>b, the Raman spectra of Sb and TiO<sub>2</sub> are presented together with Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> to clarify the peak origins. The broad peak of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> centered at 155 cm<sup>−1</sup>, arises from the overlapping of the characteristic peaks of Sb (152 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and TiO<sub>2</sub> (153 cm<sup>−1</sup>).<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0018_R_d47655007e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Phys. Rev. B">18</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0019_R_d47655007e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Sol-Gel Sci. Technol.">19</a></span> The weak peaks at 396 and 632 cm<sup>−1</sup> are definitely related to TiO<sub>2</sub>, confirming the formation of TiO<sub>2</sub> in the product. Further evidence stems from the XPS spectra of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> (Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0001">1</a>c), where the signals of Ti could be easily observed. The following deconvolution of the high-resolution spectra of Ti 2p, indicates the reduced Ti species (Ti<sup>3+</sup>) in the TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> shell (Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0001">1</a>d), which could be associated with the calcination of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> in the reducing atmosphere (Ar/H<sub>2</sub>).<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0015_R_d47655007e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0016_R_d47655007e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Mater.">16</a></span> Besides Raman and X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (XPS) spectra, the signals of TiO<sub>2</sub> could also be clearly identified in the EDS spectrum (Figure S4, Supporting Information), confirming its existence in Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201505918-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/aad9bbc2-14ef-43bb-bb6a-59ae0faa187a/adma201505918-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/aad9bbc2-14ef-43bb-bb6a-59ae0faa187a/adma201505918-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2f1cf658-58ec-4957-a047-20d929e11655/adma201505918-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/aad9bbc2-14ef-43bb-bb6a-59ae0faa187a/adma201505918-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201505918-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201505918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD pattern of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>. The standard reflections of Sb (red, JCPDS Card No. 35–0732) and TiO<sub>2</sub> (blue, JCPDS Card No. 21–1272) are presented at the bottom. b) Raman spectra of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, TiO<sub>2</sub>, and Sb. c) Survey XPS spectra of TiO<sub>2</sub> and Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>. d) High-resolution XPS spectra of Ti 2p in TiO<sub>2</sub> and Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0002">2</a> shows the typical scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>. As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0002">2</a>a,b, Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> inherits the 1D shape from Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods (Figure S2, Supporting Information). Moreover, as indicated by the yellow circles, Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> exhibits a typical characteristic of a hollow structure. Further confirmation of the hollow structure comes from the TEM images (Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0002">2</a>c,d), where high-quality nanotubes can be easily visualized in the product. These nanotubes present an average inner diameter of ≈40 nm and a uniform wall thickness of ≈6 nm, similar to the sizes of Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub>. Field-emission TEM (HRTEM) image (Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0002">2</a>e) shows that there are many randomly oriented but crystallized Sb nanocrystals in the wall of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> nanotubes. The clear lattice fringes with their spacing about 0.37 nm could be assigned to (003) planes of rhombohedral-phase Sb. EDS spectra (Figure S4, Supporting Information) reveal the content of Sb at 78 wt%, indicating a high content of Sb in the nanotubes. This result is in good agreement with the data from XPS spectra (73%) and the inductive coupled plasma atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES) technique (75%). Such a high content of Sb would benefit the realization of the high capacity of the nanotubes. Detailed element mapping reveals that both Sb and Ti are uniformly distributed throughout the nanotubes, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0002">2</a>f. However, the signals from neighboring nanocrystals might overlap with each other, such that it is difficult to identify individual nanocrystals of Sb in this figure. Such a distinctive morphology could be retained until the calcination temperature was increased to 450 °C (Figure S5, Supporting Information). The content of Sb would significantly decrease from 75 wt% at 350 °C to 58 wt% at 400 °C or 43 wt% at 450 °C based on the ICP-AES results, due to the low melting point of bulk Sb (≈630 °C).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201505918-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4ad1adc6-0800-401f-919e-d2b850b762b5/adma201505918-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4ad1adc6-0800-401f-919e-d2b850b762b5/adma201505918-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3451aa92-1cb5-4cd8-9e5f-d81877bd3594/adma201505918-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4ad1adc6-0800-401f-919e-d2b850b762b5/adma201505918-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201505918-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201505918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) SEM images, c,d) TEM images, e) HRTEM image, and f) elemental mapping of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> is evaluated as an anode material in a half-cell of LIBs. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0003">3</a>a shows cyclic voltammograms of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> during the first several cycles, where the cathodic peaks located at 0.80 and 1.73 V could be attributed to the lithiation reaction from Sb to Li<sub>3</sub>Sb and that from TiO<sub>2</sub> to Li<i><sub>x</sub></i>TiO<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0020_R_d47655007e727" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">20</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0021_R_d47655007e727" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">21</a></span> The anodic peaks centered at 1.07 and 1.99 V arise from the delithiation reactions of Li<sub>3</sub>Sb and Li<i><sub>x</sub></i>TiO<sub>2</sub>,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0023_R_d47655007e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">23</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0024_R_d47655007e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Electrochim. Acta.">24</a></span> suggesting good reversibility of these electrochemical reactions. In the subsequent cycles, the curves basically overlap with the first one, indicating the high stability of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>. Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0003">3</a>b presents the cycling performance of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> at a current density of 0.66 A g<sup>−1</sup>. It shows a high capacity retention and good stability upon cycling, which can be associated with the unique tubular structure and nanoscale size of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>. After 120 cycles, the reversible capacity could be still maintained at 547 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, close to the theoretical capacity of this nanocomposite (578.75 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> = 660 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> × 75 wt%(Sb) + 335 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> × 25 wt%(TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>)). This capacity is also much higher than those of Sb (Figure S6, Supporting Information), and TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> (Figure S7, Supporting Information). Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0003">3</a>c further demonstrates the long-term cyclability of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> at the high rates. After 1000 cycles at current densities of 3.3 and 6.6 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the reversible capacity could be remained at 445 and 424 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating the excellent electrochemical stability. To the best of our knowledge, such a long-life cyclability at a high rate has not been reported for Sb in lithium-ion batteries. All of the reported works only showed the cycling performances either at a low current density like 100 or 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, or at a high current density but for limited cycles, usually less than 150 cycles (Table S1, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201505918-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a4e48279-8c21-44d8-940b-b2a07fca7b3d/adma201505918-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a4e48279-8c21-44d8-940b-b2a07fca7b3d/adma201505918-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b1c9ce98-3a4a-4c4e-bf06-9e7a886180ba/adma201505918-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a4e48279-8c21-44d8-940b-b2a07fca7b3d/adma201505918-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201505918-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201505918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Cyclic voltammograms for Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> for first four cycles. b–e) Cycling performances (b,c) and rate capability (d,e) of the electrodes based on Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, Sb, and TiO<sub>2</sub>. f) The plots of log(<i>i<sub>p</sub></i>) to log(<i>v</i>) to get the <i>b</i> values of different cathodic/anodic peaks.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The excellent performance is also reflected on their rate performances (Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0003">3</a>d). Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> shows an outstanding capacity retention at various rates from 0.132 to 13.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Even at a high rate like 6.6 or 13.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, its capacity retention could be still preserved at 75.8% or 70.8%, which corresponds to a capacity of 526 or 488 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. As the rate returns to 0.132 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity recovers to 700 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating its good stability of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>. Compared to the theoretical capacity, the extra capacity may come from lithium insertion into acetylene black or nanopores,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0022_R_d47655007e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Electrochem. Commun.">22</a>-<a href="#adma201505918-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0025_R_d47655007e848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Mater. Chem. A">25</a></span> interfacial lithium storage,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0026_R_d47655007e850" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></span> and the formation of a polymeric gel-like film.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0027_R_d47655007e853" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">27</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0028_R_d47655007e853" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Solid State Sci.">28</a></span> This rate capability and their specific capacities are much better than many previous reports (Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0003">3</a>e).<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0012_R_d47655007e857" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A">12</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0014_R_d47655007e857" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0029_R_d47655007e857" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Power Sources">29</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0030_R_d47655007e857" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">30</a></span> For instance, Sb nanoparticles fabricated by the cathodic corrosion of bulk Sb showed a specific capacity of ≈422 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at ≈2 A g<sup>−1</sup> in its rate test.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0029_R_d47655007e863" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Power Sources">29</a></span> Hollow Sb nanospheres synthesized by a galvanic replacement reaction, presented a similar capacity about ≈435 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at ≈1.6 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0030_R_d47655007e870" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">30</a></span> This capacity dropped to 317 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 2.64 A g<sup>−1</sup> for the Sb/C nanocomposite prepared by a distinct magneto-ball mill.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0012_R_d47655007e876" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Mater. Chem. A">12</a></span> So far, only one report about Sb nanoparticles showed comparable electrochemical properties.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0031_R_d47655007e879" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Lett.">31</a></span> Figure S8 of the Supporting Information shows the cyclic voltammograms of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> at different scan rates, which could be used to deduce the capacitance contribution in the reaction based on the slope (i.e., <i>b</i> value) calculated by the plots of log(<i>i<sub>p</sub></i>) to log(<i>v</i>).<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0032_R_d47655007e894" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></span> In general, <i>b</i> = 1 indicates that the electrochemical reaction is surface-limited, corresponding to a capacitive behaviour. <i>b</i> = 0.5 implies that it is a diffusion-limited electrochemical reaction, caused by Li-ion insertion/extraction. Usually, <i>b</i> values are located between 0.5–1, indicating both of them contribute to the reaction. Moreover, they differ a lot, not only for different materials, but also for different peaks in the same material, which has been observed in many reports.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0032_R_d47655007e902" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a>-<a href="#adma201505918-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0034_R_d47655007e902" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nat. Commun.">34</a></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0003">3</a>f, TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> exhibits higher <i>b</i> values than Sb for both cathodic and anodic peaks, indicating its capability for the fast storage/releasing of lithium ions. Associated with the configuration of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, this result would benefit the electrochemical performances of the entire composite, particularly at a high current density.</p>
         
         <p>In view of its excellent performances in half cells, Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> is further examined in full cells with commercial LiCoO<sub>2</sub> powders as the cathode material (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0004">4</a>a), which has not been explored before. Before that, the commercial LiCoO<sub>2</sub> powders are first checked in half cells, as shown in Figure S9 of the Supporting Information. It delivers a reversible capacity of ≈155 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. As the substantial charge/discharge plateaus of LiCoO<sub>2</sub> are around 3.9/3.9 V (Figure S9b, Supporting Information) and those of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> are at 0.8/1.1 V, the voltage window for Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>//LiCoO<sub>2</sub> is set between 1.4 and 3.8 V for the following tests of full cells. The output voltage of this full cell is high enough to turn on a light-emitting diode (LED) (2 V, 20 mW, Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0004">4</a>a). The plots of d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> vs voltage for the first several cycles of the full cell are presented in Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0004">4</a>b. The intense cathodic and anodic peaks at 3.18 and 3.30 V correspond to the lithiation of Sb and the delithiation of Li<sub>3</sub>Sb. The weak peaks at 2.32 and 2.08 V (the inset of Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0004">4</a>b) may be related to the insertion of lithium into TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> or the extraction from Li<i><sub>x</sub></i>TiO<sub>2</sub>. The following cycles basically repeat the curves, indicating the good stability of electrode materials. Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0004">4</a>c shows the cycling performance of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>//LiCoO<sub>2</sub> at a current density of 3.3 A g<sub>anode</sub><sup>−1</sup>, where the gravimetric capacity is calculated by the mass of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>. After 200 cycles, the reversible capacity could be still kept at 458 mA h g<sup>−1</sup><sub>anode</sub>. The excellent electrochemical stability of this full cell is also reflected in the rate performance, as shown in Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0004">4</a>d. The reversible capacities of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> at 0.132, 0.33, 0.66, 1.32, 3.3, and 6.6 A g<sup>−1</sup> could be preserved at 642, 611, 577, 554, 536, and 484 mA h g<sup>−1</sup><sub>anode</sub>, close to those in half cells. Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0004">4</a>e presents the typical charge/discharge profiles at these rates, where the gravimetric capacity is recalculated based on the total mass of LiCoO<sub>2</sub> and Sb@TiO<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0035_R_d47655007e1011" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Nano">35</a></span> Although the rate greatly increases, there is only a slight decrease in the reversible capacities and voltage plateaus (≈0.06 V, the inset of Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0004">4</a>f), indicating the good reaction kinetics. The Ragone plot (Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0004">4</a>f) was measured to display the energy density and power density of the full cell.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0035_R_d47655007e1018" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Nano">35</a></span> At a low power density of 77 W kg<sup>−1</sup><sub>total</sub>, the cell shows a high energy density of 360 W h kg<sup>−1</sup><sub>total</sub>. As the power density increases to 3.77 kW kg<sup>−1</sup><sub>total</sub>, the cell still keeps an energy density of ≈262 W h kg<sup>−1</sup><sub>total</sub>. Moreover, as the current density greatly increases, the discharge plateau only drops a little (the inset of Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0004">4</a>f), suggesting the small polarization and good kinetics in full cells. It should be pointed that both the cutoff voltage and mass loading here are different from those in commercial batteries. The lower cutoff voltage and less mass loading here, compared to those for commercial batteries, are to fully activate the electrode materials, Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, and LiCoO<sub>2</sub>. In spite of this, the comparison between different electrode materials is reliable, because they were used to fabricate the electrode based on the same protocol and tested under the same conditions.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201505918-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3edb9146-818b-4285-8d3c-585888497d21/adma201505918-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3edb9146-818b-4285-8d3c-585888497d21/adma201505918-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/555748b4-c588-4bda-89cc-71a9dd99850f/adma201505918-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3edb9146-818b-4285-8d3c-585888497d21/adma201505918-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201505918-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201505918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Digital image of the light-emitting diode lit by Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>//LiCoO<sub>2</sub> full batteries. b) Corresponding capacity differential curves,c) cycling performance at 3.3 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and (d) rate performance of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>//LiCoO<sub>2</sub> full batteries, where only the mass of anode, Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, is taken into account to calculate current density and gravimetric capacity. e) Discharge/charge curves at different rates and f) Ragone plot of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>//LiCoO<sub>2</sub> full batteries, where gravimetric capacity, power, and energy density are estimated based on the total masses of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> and LiCoO<sub>2</sub>. The inset of (f) is the average discharge voltage at different rates of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>//LiCoO<sub>2</sub> full batteries.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Because Sb is also known to be a promising anode candidate for NIBs,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0036_R_d47655007e1098" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Energy Environ. Sci.">36</a></span> the sodium-storage performances of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> are also evaluated in half cells first and then in full cells, like the case in LIBs. First of all, the effect of fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) on the electrochemical properties of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> in NIBs (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0005">5</a>a) is investigated in two electrolytes, diethyl carbonate (DEC)/ethylene carbonate (EC) and propylene carbonate (PC). It is noticed that the addition of FEC substantially improves the capacities in both electrolytes. The maximum (≈520 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) appears in the electrolyte containing 10% of FEC, consistent with previous reports<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0031_R_d47655007e1116" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Lett.">31</a></span> and close to the theoretical capacity (578.75 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> = 660 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> × 75 wt%(Sb) + 335 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> × 25 wt%(TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>)) of such mixture Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>. Compared with EC/DEC, PC is particularly good at high rates (Figure S10, Supporting Information). Thus, all the following measurements are conducted in PC containing 10% FEC. Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0005">5</a>b shows the CV curves of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> for the first several cycles. The cathodic peak at 0.35 V can be ascribed to the insertion of Na ions into TiO<sub>2</sub> and Sb.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0031_R_d47655007e1142" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Lett.">31</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0037_R_d47655007e1142" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Commun.">37</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0038_R_d47655007e1142" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></span> The anodic peak at 0.88 V stems from the extraction of Na ions from Na<sub>3</sub>Sb and Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TiO<sub>2</sub>.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0031_R_d47655007e1151" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Lett.">31</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0038_R_d47655007e1151" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></span> After the first cycle, the broad cathodic peak over 0.7–1.5 V vanishes from the profile, indicating that it corresponds to the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0037_R_d47655007e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Chem. Commun.">37</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0038_R_d47655007e1154" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201505918-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/90051704-7a8a-49f8-a81e-3ff2624abcd7/adma201505918-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/90051704-7a8a-49f8-a81e-3ff2624abcd7/adma201505918-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/25225a0c-2ef8-44b5-a5f3-cd63ce100c33/adma201505918-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/90051704-7a8a-49f8-a81e-3ff2624abcd7/adma201505918-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201505918-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201505918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The influence of FEC in different electrolytes on the electrochemical performance of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>. b) CV curves of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> of initial four cycles. c) Galvanostatic discharge–charge cycles at 2.64 A g<sup>−1</sup> and d,e) rate performance of electrodes based on Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, Sb, and TiO<sub>2</sub>. f) The plots of log(<i>i<sub>p</sub></i>) to log(<i>v</i>) for cathodic/anodic peaks.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The cycling performance of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> is measured at a current density of 2.64 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0005">5</a>c, Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> exhibits capacities close to Sb nanoparticles before 180 cycles, both of which are much higher than TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>. The capacity of Sb nanoparticles quickly decays from the 180th to the 220th cycle, which suggests that even Sb nanoparticles cannot stand with the huge volume change after prolonged cycling. However, that of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> only drops slightly and stays at ≈300 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles, much higher than those of Sb nanoparticles (≈20 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) and TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> (≈130 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>). This result clearly confirms the importance of TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> to stabilize the sodium-storage performance of Sb. This result is the longest cycle life at such a high rate, to our knowledge. Even for high-quality Sb nanoparticles, the cycling at 2.64 A g<sup>−1</sup> was conducted only for 100 cycles.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0031_R_d47655007e1238" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Lett.">31</a></span> The outstanding performance of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> is also supported by the rate measurements (Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0005">5</a>d). Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> exhibits reversible capacities of 601, 516, 469, 440, 417, 388, and 312 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at rates of 0.066, 0.33, 0.66, 1.32, 3.3, 6.6, and 13.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, much higher than those of Sb and TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, especially at high rates. Once the rate goes back to 0.066 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity could be also recovered completely. These results are much better than most of the previous reports (Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0005">5</a>e, and Table S2, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0010_R_d47655007e1264" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Commun.">10</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0011_R_d47655007e1264" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Environ. Sci.">11</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0039_R_d47655007e1264" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Power Sources">39</a>-<a href="#adma201505918-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0044_R_d47655007e1264" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Mater. Chem. A">44</a></span> Sb/C nanofibers fabricated by the electrospinning technique and high-temperature calcination, showed a capacity of 337 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 3 A g<sup>−1</sup> in the rate-capability test.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0011_R_d47655007e1271" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Energy Environ. Sci.">11</a></span> Such performances were also observed for Sb/C nanofibers by thermal carbonization (325 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 3.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>),<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0039_R_d47655007e1277" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Power Sources">39</a></span> for Sb@C microspheres synthesized by a spray-drying process (302 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>),<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0040_R_d47655007e1284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Mater. Chem. A">40</a></span> for hollow Sb microspheres prepared by a galvanic replacement reaction (313 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 3.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>),<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0041_R_d47655007e1290" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Mater. Chem. A">41</a></span> and for porous Sb/Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> nanocomposite (299 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 3.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0042_R_d47655007e1301" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Small">42</a></span> The rate capability was even worse in Sb/C microspheres (≈267 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>),<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0010_R_d47655007e1308" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Commun.">10</a></span> Sb/carbon nanosheets (≈200 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>),<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0043_R_d47655007e1314" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Chem. Commun.">43</a></span> and Sb/reduced graphene oxide (≈192 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 3.3 A g<sup>−1</sup>).<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0044_R_d47655007e1321" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Mater. Chem. A">44</a></span> It should be pointed that the rate capability of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> in NIBs is worse than that in LIBs, which could be associated with the highly inhomogeneous sodiation of Sb even in nanoscale structures.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0045_R_d47655007e1327" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Nano Lett.">45</a></span> This result indicates the great intrinsic stress in Sb, then accelerating the electrode pulverization upon cycling. Thus, the capacity of pure Sb drops quickly in NIBs. Meanwhile, the large diameter of sodium (vs lithium) results in a large volume change and sluggish transportation, deteriorating the rate capability and cycling stability. Like the case of LIBs, the CV curves of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> at different scan rates (Figure S11, Supporting Information) are measured to identify the capacitance contribution for sodium storage. Then, log(<i>i</i>) is plotted against log(<i>v</i>) to estimate the <i>b</i> values. It is found that the <i>b</i> value is 0.66 for the cathodic peak and 0.78 for the anodic, both of which indicate the capacitances for the sodium-storage reactions. This fact explains the excellent rate performances of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>. Usually, the greater the capacitance is, the better the rate capability.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0032_R_d47655007e1346" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></span></p>
         
         <p>The excellent sodium-storage performance of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> in a half cell inspires our moving to full cells (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0006">6</a>). Na-ion full cells are rarely investigated, due to their limited cycle life and low reversible capacity. Here, home-made Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C nanocomposites are employed as the cathode material for Na-ion full cells,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0046_R_d47655007e1368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Energy Mater.">46</a></span> which could stabilize at a specific capacity of 90 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 20 cycles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure S12, Supporting Information). The full battery was tested within 1.2–3.4 V at a current density of 660 mA g<sup>−1</sup><sub>anode</sub>. There is only one plateau at 2.5 V, much higher than Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//MoO<sub>3</sub> (1.4 V),<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0022_R_d47655007e1389" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Electrochem. Commun.">22</a></span> Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//FeSe<sub>2</sub> (1.7 V)<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0032_R_d47655007e1402" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></span> and approximately to Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (2.8 V).<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0047_R_d47655007e1421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Energy Environ. Sci.">47</a></span> This would benefit its following applications in practice. The full cell is quite stable upon cycling. Even after 100 cycles at a current density of 660 mA g<sup>−1</sup><sub>anode</sub>, the full cell still remains a capacity of 350 mA h g<sup>−1</sup><sub>anode</sub> (Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0006">6</a>c). The capacity loss during the first tens of cycles might be related to the formation of SEI film and irreversible consummation of sodium ions by Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C cathode.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0022_R_d47655007e1441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Electrochem. Commun.">22</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0032_R_d47655007e1441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0047_R_d47655007e1441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Energy Environ. Sci.">47</a></span> Even at 6.6 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the full cell still maintains a capacity of 310 mA h g<sup>−1</sup><sub>anode</sub> (Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0006">6</a>d). Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0006">6</a>e shows the discharge–charge curves of Na-ion full batteries at different rates. Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>-C battery displays a larger polarization and a lower discharge plateau, compared to the Li-ion full batteries. However, its energy density and power density (Figure <a href="#adma201505918-fig-0006">6</a>f) are still higher than the reported Na-ion full batteries.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0022_R_d47655007e1468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Electrochem. Commun.">22</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0032_R_d47655007e1468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0047_R_d47655007e1468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Energy Environ. Sci.">47</a></span></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma201505918-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/48100b88-30a4-4ba0-89ac-481fbdfa2934/adma201505918-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/48100b88-30a4-4ba0-89ac-481fbdfa2934/adma201505918-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/804d5a05-82d9-412e-8c92-2dcf3006c726/adma201505918-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/48100b88-30a4-4ba0-89ac-481fbdfa2934/adma201505918-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma201505918-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.201505918" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Digital image of the light-emitting diode lit by Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>-C full cells, the inset is the schematic image of the full battery. b) Discharge/charge curves, c) cycling performance at 0.66 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and d) rate performance of this full battery, where only the mass of anode, Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, is taken into account to calculate current density and gravimetric capacity. e) Discharge/charge curves at different rates and f) Ragone plot of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>-C full batteries, where gravimetric capacity, power, and energy density are estimated based on the total masses of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>-C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The excellent electrochemical properties of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> originate from the synergistic effect of nanoscale size, tubular structure, and distinctive interaction of Sb/TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, by which the morphology can be basically kept during cycling (Figure S13, Supporting Information). The nanoscale size of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> effectively shortens the diffusion pathway of lithium ions/sodium ions/electrons, reduces the volume change upon cycling, and lowers the areal current density, benefiting the promotion of structure stability and electrochemical kinetics.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0048_R_d47655007e1545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a></span> The tubular structure of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> offers plenty of room to accommodate the volume change and simultaneously improves the contact between the active material and the electrolyte.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0044_R_d47655007e1552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Mater. Chem. A">44</a></span> Furthermore, the 1D shape is also thought to be helpful for the charge transfer during electrochemical progress.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0049_R_d47655007e1554" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Science">49</a></span> Finally, the unique interaction of Sb/TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> attracts the newly formed Sb nanoparticles to the shell, and avoids the evaporation of Sb during the calcination, realizing the high-content Sb in the double-walled nanotubes. Meanwhile, this interaction would also prevent the agglomeration of Sb upon the cycling, thereby benefiting the stable electrochemical properties. In addition, the amorphous structure and oxygen vacancies of TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> reduce the charge-transfer resistance between electrolyte and electrode, and increase the electron conductivity,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0015_R_d47655007e1565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></span> both of which make it a robust interface material for high-performance anode materials. These conclusions are supported by electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) (Figure S14, Supporting Information). Although Sb presents the smallest charge-transfer resistances (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) in LIBs (89 Ω) and in NIBs (122 Ω) at the beginning of the cycles, these values greatly rise to 926 Ω in LIBs and 1003 Ω in NIBs after hundreds of cycles. Compared to the case of Sb, Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>, and TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> show the excellent stability. Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> is even better than TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> in terms of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, no matter at the beginning of cycles or after hundreds of cycles. These results indicate the importance of TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> to Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>.</p>
         
         <p>In summary, double-walled Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> nanotubes are successfully synthesized to confirm the enhancement of TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> to alloy-type anode materials. This structure takes the advantages of excellent electrochemical stability of TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> and high capacity of Sb together. Together with its nanoscale size and hollow structure, it shows outstanding electrochemical properties, not only in lithium-ion batteries but also in sodium-ion batteries. It exhibits a reversible capacity of 424 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 6.6 A g<sup>−1</sup> in a half cell of lithium-ion batteries or ≈300 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 2.64 A g<sup>−1</sup> in a half cell of sodium-ion batteries. In the full cell, the energy density of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>//LiCoO<sub>2</sub> is approximately ≈360 W h kg<sup>−1</sup> at 77 W kg<sup>−1</sup>. Even at 3.77 kW kg<sup>−1</sup>, there is still about 262 W h kg<sup>−1</sup>. In the full cell of Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>//Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>-C, the energy density turns to be 151 W h kg<sup>−1</sup> at 21 W kg<sup>−1</sup>, or 61 W h kg<sup>−1</sup> at 1.83 kW kg<sup>−1</sup>. All these data indicate the promising potential of TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> as an interface material for high-performance anode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma201505918-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma201505918-sec-0030-title"> Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Material Synthesis</i>: All the reagents were used without any further purification after purchase. First, 2 mmol Na<sub>2</sub>S·9H<sub>2</sub>O was dissolved in 20 mL of ethylene glycol. Then, 1 mmol SbCl<sub>3</sub> dissolved in another 20 mL of ethylene glycol was added dropwise in the above solution under stirring. The solution was transferred to a Teflon-lined autoclave and kept at 200 °C for 10 h. The product, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanorods, was filtrated and washed with deionized water and ethanol for three times, afterward dried under vacuum at 60 °C overnight. Then, Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanorods were dispersed in 40 mL of ethanol by sonicating, followed with the addition of 0.2 mL of NH<sub>3</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O and 0.4 mL of TBOT. The reaction was conducted at 45 °C for 12 h, generating core–shell Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods. The core–shell nanorods were calcined at 350 °C for 10 h in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> (Vol: 95%: 5%),resulting in double-walled Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i> nanotubes.</p>
         
         <p><i>Sample Characterization</i>: The morphology and composition of the samples were characterized using an X-ray diffractometer (Bruker D8 Adv, Germany), a transmission electron microscope (JEOL JEM 1011, Japan), a high-resolution transmission electron microscope (JEOL 2100, Japan), a field-emission scanning electron microscope (SUPRA 55, Germany), an X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (ESCALAB 250, USA), an inductive coupled plasma atomic emission spectrometer (IRIS Intrepid II XSP, USA), and a thermal analyzer (Mettler Toledo TGA/SDTA 851, Switzerland). Electrochemical impedance spectra were measured using an AUTOLAB PGSTAT302 electrochemical workstation in the frequency range of 100 kHz to 0.01 Hz. ICP-AES (IRIS Inrtrepid II XSP) was used to analyze the chemical composition of the products.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurements</i>: The lithium-storage performances of the samples were tested with CR2032 coin cells, in which 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> LiPF<sub>6</sub> in dimethyl carbonate (DMC), DEC, and EC (volume ratio of 1:1:1) was the electrolyte, a Celgard 2400 membrane was the separator, and Li foil was the counter and reference electrode. Working electrodes were made of 70 wt% of active materials (Sb, TiO<sub>2</sub>, or Sb@TiO<sub>2−</sub><i><sub>x</sub></i>), 20 wt% of acetylene black, and 10 wt% of carboxymethyl cellulose sodium (CMC). The blend was milled for 30 min with a trace of deionized water to get a slurry. The slurry was then spread on a copper foil using a blade and dried overnight in vacuum at 60 °C. The typical mass loading of active materials was controlled at 1–1.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The coated copper foil was roll-pressed and punched into discs as working electrodes. The disks were assembled with the counter electrode, the separator, the electrolyte, in a glove box filled with argon (Mikrouna, Super 1220/750/900). The electrochemical properties of the cells were evaluated by cyclic voltammetry (CV) on electrochemical workstations (LK 2005A, China). The lithium-storage performances such as cycling stability and rate capability, were characterized by galvanostatic discharge–charge cyclers (LAND CT-2001A, China). In the case of Li-ion full cell, commercial LiCoO<sub>2</sub>, acetylene black and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) in a weight ratio of 8:1:1, were blended and dried on an Al foil to form the cathode electrode. The anode was electrochemically activated for three cycles to eliminate the huge capacity loss in the first cycle, prior to the use in full cell.<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0050_R_d47655007e1729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Power Sources">50</a>, <a href="#adma201505918-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0051_R_d47655007e1729" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem. Mater.">51</a></span></p>
         
         <p>In the case of NIBs, the half cells were constructed from sodium foils as the counter/reference electrode, glass microfibers of Whatman GF/F as the separator, and 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC and DEC (volume ratio, 1:1) or 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in PC containing an appropriate amount of FEC as the electrolyte. For Na-ion full cells, the cathode was composed of home-made Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>/C,<span><a href="#adma201505918-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma201505918-bib-0046_R_d47655007e1750" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Energy Mater.">46</a></span> acetylene black, and PVDF in a weight ratio of 75:15:10. Similar to the case of LIBs, the anodes used in the full cell of NIBs were also cycled in a half cell, before they were assembled into the full cells.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

               <div class="support-info__table-wrapper article-table-content-wrapper">
                  <p>As a service to our authors and readers, this journal provides supporting information supplied by the authors. Such materials are peer reviewed and may be re-organized for online delivery, but are not copy-edited or typeset. Technical support issues arising from supporting information (other than missing files) should be addressed to the authors.</p>
